highlighted many instances in which Lenin displayed kindness, particularly toward children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead2005259_464-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead2005259-464">[461]</a></sup> According to several biographers, Lenin was intolerant of opposition and often dismissed outright opinions that differed from his own.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196467Pipes1990353Read2005207,_212_465-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196467Pipes1990353Read2005207,_212-465">[462]</a></sup> He could be "venomous in his critique of others", exhibiting a propensity for mockery, ridicule, and _ad hominem_ attacks on those who disagreed with him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern201093_466-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern201093-466">[463]</a></sup> He ignored facts that did not suit his argument,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes1990353_467-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes1990353-467">[464]</a></sup> abhorred compromise,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196469_468-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196469-468">[465]</a></sup> and very rarely admitted his own errors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000244Read2005153_469-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000244Read2005153-469">[466]</a></sup> He refused to change his opinions, until he rejected them completely, after which he would treat the new view as if it was just as unchangeable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196459_470-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196459-470">[467]</a></sup> Lenin showed no sign of [sadism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sadistic_personality_disorder "Sadistic personality disorder") or of personally desiring to commit violent acts, but he endorsed the violent actions of others and exhibited no remorse for those killed for the revolutionary cause.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196445Pipes1990350Volkogonov1994182Service2000177Read2005208Ryan20126_471-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196445Pipes1990350Volkogonov1994182Service2000177Read2005208Ryan20126-471">[468]</a></sup> Adopting a [utilitarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utilitarian "Utilitarian") stance, in Lenin's view the end always justified the means;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964415Shub1966422Read2005247_472-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964415Shub1966422Read2005247-472">[469]</a></sup> according to Service, Lenin's "criterion of morality was simple: does a certain action advance or hinder the cause of the Revolution?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000293_473-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000293-473">[470]</a></sup>

Ethnically, Lenin identified as Russian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern201067_474-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern201067-474">[471]</a></sup> Service described Lenin as "a bit of a snob in national, social and cultural terms".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000453_475-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000453-475">[472]</a></sup> The Bolshevik leader believed that other European countries, especially Germany, were culturally superior to Russia,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000389_476-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000389-476">[473]</a></sup> describing the latter as "one of the most benighted, medieval and shamefully backward of Asian countries".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice1990121_433-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice1990121-433">[430]</a></sup> He was annoyed at what he perceived as a lack of conscientiousness and discipline among the Russian people, and from his youth had wanted Russia to become more culturally European and Western.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes199611Service2000389–400_477-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes199611Service2000389%E2%80%93400-477">[474]</a></sup>

> The Lenin who seemed externally so gentle and good-natured, who enjoyed a laugh, who loved animals and was prone to sentimental reminiscences, was transformed when class or political questions arose. He at once became savagely sharp, uncompromising, remorseless and vengeful. Even in such a state he was capable of [black humour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_humour "Black humour").

—Biographer Dmitri Volkogonov, 1994<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994200_478-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994200-478">[475]</a></sup>

Despite his revolutionary politics, Lenin disliked revolutionary experimentation in literature and the arts, expressing his dislike of [expressionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expressionism "Expressionism"), [futurism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Futurism "Futurism"), and [cubism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cubism "Cubism"), and conversely favouring [realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realism_(arts) "Realism (arts)") and Russian [classic literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classic_literature "Classic literature").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964489,_491Shub1966420–421Sandle1999125Read2005237_479-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964489,_491Shub1966420%E2%80%93421Sandle1999125Read2005237-479">[476]</a></sup> Lenin also had a conservative attitude towards sex and marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196479Read2005237_480-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196479Read2005237-480">[477]</a></sup> Throughout his adult life, he was in a relationship with Krupskaya, a fellow Marxist whom he married. Lenin and Krupskaya both regretted that they never had children,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000199_481-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000199-481">[478]</a></sup> and they enjoyed entertaining their friends' offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShub1966424Service2000213Rappaport201038_482-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShub1966424Service2000213Rappaport201038-482">[479]</a></sup> Read noted that Lenin had "very close, warm, lifelong relationships" with his close family members;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead200519_483-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead200519-483">[480]</a></sup> he had no lifelong friends, and Armand has been cited as being his only close, intimate confidante.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964515Volkogonov1994246_484-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964515Volkogonov1994246-484">[481]</a></sup>

Aside from Russian, Lenin spoke and read French, German, and English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000242_485-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000242-485">[482]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-486"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-486">[483]</a></sup> Concerned with physical fitness, he exercised regularly,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196456Rice1990106Service2000160_487-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196456Rice1990106Service2000160-487">[484]</a></sup> enjoyed cycling, swimming, and hunting,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer196456Service2000188_488-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer196456Service2000188-488">[485]</a></sup> and also developed a passion for mountain walking in the Swiss peaks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead200520,_64,_132–37_489-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead200520,_64,_132%E2%80%9337-489">[486]</a></sup> He was also fond of pets,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShub1966423_490-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShub1966423-490">[487]</a></sup> in particular cats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964367_491-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964367-491">[488]</a></sup> Tending to eschew luxury, he lived a spartan lifestyle,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964368_492-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964368-492">[489]</a></sup> and Pipes noted that Lenin was "exceedingly modest in his personal wants", leading "an austere, almost ascetic, style of life."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes1990812_493-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes1990812-493">[490]</a></sup> Lenin despised untidiness, always keeping his work desk tidy and his pencils sharpened, and insisted on total silence while he was working.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService200099–100,_160_494-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService200099%E2%80%93100,_160-494">[491]</a></sup> According to Fischer, Lenin's "vanity was minimal",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964245_495-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964245-495">[492]</a></sup> and for this reason he disliked the [cult of personality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cult_of_personality "Cult of personality") that the Soviet administration began to build around him; he nevertheless accepted that it might have some benefits in unifying the communist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes1990349–350Read2005284,_259–260_496-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes1990349%E2%80%93350Read2005284,_259%E2%80%93260-496">[493]</a></sup>

## Death and funeral: 1923–1924

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Lenin%27s_funerals_by_I.Brodsky_%281925%29_detail_01.jpg/220px-Lenin%27s_funerals_by_I.Brodsky_%281925%29_detail_01.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lenin%27s_funerals_by_I.Brodsky_(1925)_detail_01.jpg)

Lenin's funeral, as painted by [Isaac Brodsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Brodsky "Isaac Brodsky"), 1925

In March 1923, Lenin had a third stroke and lost his ability to speak;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964671Shub1966436Lewin1969103Leggett1981355Rice1990193White2001176Read2005281_497-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964671Shub1966436Lewin1969103Leggett1981355Rice1990193White2001176Read2005281-497">[494]</a></sup> that month, he experienced partial paralysis on his right side and began exhibiting [sensory aphasia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sensory_aphasia "Sensory aphasia").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964671Shub1966436Volkogonov1994425Service2000474LernerFinkelsteinWitztum2004372_498-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964671Shub1966436Volkogonov1994425Service2000474LernerFinkelsteinWitztum2004372-498">[495]</a></sup> By May, he appeared to be making a slow recovery, regaining some of his mobility, speech, and writing skills.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964672Rigby1979192Rice1990193–194Volkogonov1994429–430_499-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964672Rigby1979192Rice1990193%E2%80%93194Volkogonov1994429%E2%80%93430-499">[496]</a></sup> In October, he made a final visit to the Kremlin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964672Shub1966437Volkogonov1994431Service2000476Read2005281_500-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964672Shub1966437Volkogonov1994431Service2000476Read2005281-500">[497]</a></sup> In his final weeks, Lenin was visited by Zinoviev, Kamenev, and Bukharin; the latter visited him at his Gorki mansion on the day of his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice1990194Volkogonov1994299Service2000477–478_501-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice1990194Volkogonov1994299Service2000477%E2%80%93478-501">[498]</a></sup> On 21 January 1924, Lenin fell into a [coma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coma "Coma") and died later that day at age 53.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964673–674Shub1966438Rice1990194Volkogonov1994435Service2000478–479White2001176Read2005269_502-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964673%E2%80%93674Shub1966438Rice1990194Volkogonov1994435Service2000478%E2%80%93479White2001176Read2005269-502">[499]</a></sup> His official cause of death was recorded as an incurable disease of the blood vessels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994435LernerFinkelsteinWitztum2004372_503-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994435LernerFinkelsteinWitztum2004372-503">[500]</a></sup>

The Soviet government publicly announced Lenin's death the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice19907_504-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice19907-504">[501]</a></sup> On 23 January, mourners from the Communist Party, trade unions, and Soviets visited his Gorki home to inspect the body, which was carried aloft in a red coffin by leading Bolsheviks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice19907–8_505-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice19907%E2%80%938-505">[502]</a></sup> Transported by train to Moscow, the coffin was taken to the [House of Trade Unions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Trade_Unions "House of Trade Unions"), where the body [lay in state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lay_in_state "Lay in state").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964674Shub1966439Rice19907–8Service2000479_506-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964674Shub1966439Rice19907%E2%80%938Service2000479-506">[503]</a></sup> Over the next three days, around a million mourners came to see the body, many queuing for hours in the freezing conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice19909_507-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice19909-507">[504]</a></sup> On 26 January, the eleventh All-Union Congress of Soviets met to pay respects, with speeches by Kalinin, Zinoviev, and Stalin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice19909_507-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice19909-507">[504]</a></sup> Notably, Trotsky was absent; he had been convalescing in the Caucasus, and he later claimed that Stalin sent him a telegram with the incorrect date of the planned funeral, making it impossible for him to arrive in time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''History'',_April_2009_508-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''History'',_April_2009-508">[505]</a></sup> Lenin's funeral took place the following day, when his body was carried to [Red Square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Square "Red Square"), accompanied by martial music, where assembled crowds listened to a series of speeches before the corpse was placed into the vault of a specially erected mausoleum.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShub1966439Rice19909Service2000479–480_509-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShub1966439Rice19909Service2000479%E2%80%93480-509">[506]</a></sup> Despite the freezing temperatures, tens of thousands attended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994440_510-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994440-510">[507]</a></sup>

Against Krupskaya's protestations, Lenin's body was embalmed to preserve it for long-term public display in the Red Square mausoleum.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964674Shub1966438Volkogonov1994437–438Service2000481_511-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964674Shub1966438Volkogonov1994437%E2%80%93438Service2000481-511">[508]</a></sup> During this process, Lenin's brain was removed; in 1925 an institute was established to dissect it, revealing that Lenin had had severe [sclerosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sclerosis_(medicine) "Sclerosis (medicine)").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964625–626Volkogonov1994446_512-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964625%E2%80%93626Volkogonov1994446-512">[509]</a></sup> In July 1929, the Politburo agreed to replace the temporary mausoleum with a permanent one in granite, which was finished in 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994444,_445_513-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994444,_445-513">[510]</a></sup> His sarcophagus was replaced in 1940 and again in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994445_514-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994445-514">[511]</a></sup> For safety amid the [Second World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_World_War "Second World War"), from 1941 to 1945 the body was temporarily moved to [Tyumen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyumen "Tyumen").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994444_515-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994444-515">[512]</a></sup> As of 2023, his body remains on public display in [Lenin's Mausoleum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin%27s_Mausoleum "Lenin's Mausoleum") on Red Square.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoscow.info_516-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoscow.info-516">[513]</a></sup>

## Legacy

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Statue_of_Lenin_in_2003%2C_Hanoi_2.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Lenin_in_2003%2C_Hanoi_2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Statue_of_Lenin_in_2003,_Hanoi_2.jpg)

Lenin statue in [Hanoi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanoi "Hanoi"), [Vietnam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnam "Vietnam")

Volkogonov said, while renouncing Leninist ideology, that "there can scarcely have been another man in history who managed so profoundly to change so large a society on such a scale."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994326_517-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994326-517">[514]</a></sup> Lenin's administration laid the framework for the system of government that ruled Russia for seven decades and provided the model for later Communist-led states that came to cover a third of the inhabited world in the mid-20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000391_518-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000391-518">[515]</a></sup> As a result, Lenin's influence was global.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994259_519-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994259-519">[516]</a></sup> A controversial figure, Lenin remains both reviled and revered,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan20123_431-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan20123-431">[428]</a></sup> a figure who has been both idolised and demonised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead2005284_520-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead2005284-520">[517]</a></sup> Even during his lifetime, Lenin "was loved and hated, admired and scorned" by the Russian people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964414_521-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964414-521">[518]</a></sup> This has extended into academic studies of Lenin and Leninism, which have often been polarised along political lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiebman197519–20_522-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiebman197519%E2%80%9320-522">[519]</a></sup>

The historian [Albert Resis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Resis "Albert Resis") suggested that if the October Revolution is considered the most significant event of the 20th century, then Lenin "must for good or ill be considered the century's most significant political leader."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Encyclopedia_Britannica''_523-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Encyclopedia_Britannica''-523">[520]</a></sup> White described Lenin as "one of the undeniably outstanding figures of modern history",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2001iix_524-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2001iix-524">[521]</a></sup> while Service noted that the Russian leader was widely understood to be one of the 20th century's "principal actors."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000488_525-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000488-525">[522]</a></sup> Read considered him "one of the most widespread, universally recognizable icons of the twentieth century",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead2005283_526-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead2005283-526">[523]</a></sup> while Ryan called him "one of the most significant and influential figures of modern history."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan20125_527-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan20125-527">[524]</a></sup> _Time_ magazine named Lenin one of the [100 most important people of the 20th century](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century "Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_13_April_1998_528-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_13_April_1998-528">[525]</a></sup> and one of their top 25 political icons of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_4_February_2011_529-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_4_February_2011-529">[526]</a></sup>

In the Western world, biographers began writing about Lenin soon after his death; some such as [Christopher Hill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Hill_(historian) "Christopher Hill (historian)") were sympathetic to him, and others such as Richard Pipes and [Robert Gellately](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Gellately "Robert Gellately") expressly hostile. Some later biographers such as Read and Lars Lih sought to avoid making either hostile or positive comments about him, thereby evading politicised stereotypes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200314Ryan20123_530-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200314Ryan20123-530">[527]</a></sup> Among sympathisers, he was portrayed as having made a genuine adjustment of Marxist theory that enabled it to suit Russia's particular socio-economic conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200314_531-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200314-531">[528]</a></sup> The Soviet view characterised him as a man who recognised the historically inevitable and accordingly helped to make the inevitable happen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003123_532-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003123-532">[529]</a></sup> Conversely, the majority of Western historians have perceived him as a person who manipulated events in order to attain and then retain political power, moreover, considering his ideas as attempts to ideologically justify his pragmatic policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003123_532-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003123-532">[529]</a></sup> Later, [revisionists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_revisionism "Historical revisionism") in both Russia and the West highlighted the impact that pre-existing ideas and popular pressures exerted on Lenin and his policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003124_533-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003124-533">[530]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Lenin-statue-in-Berlin.jpg/170px-Lenin-statue-in-Berlin.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lenin-statue-in-Berlin.jpg)

Statue of Lenin erected by the East German Marxist–Leninist government at Leninplatz in [East Berlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Berlin "East Berlin") (removed in 1992)

Various historians and biographers have characterised Lenin's administration as [totalitarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Totalitarian "Totalitarian"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964516Shub1966415Leggett1981364Volkogonov1994307,_312_534-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964516Shub1966415Leggett1981364Volkogonov1994307,_312-534">[531]</a></sup> and as a [police state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Police_state "Police state"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeggett1981364_535-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeggett1981364-535">[532]</a></sup> and many have described it as a one-party dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewin196912Rigby1979x,_161Sandle1999164Service2000506Lee200397Read2005190Ryan20129_536-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewin196912Rigby1979x,_161Sandle1999164Service2000506Lee200397Read2005190Ryan20129-536">[533]</a></sup> Several such scholars have described Lenin as a dictator;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964417Shub1966416Pipes1990511Pipes19963Read2005247_537-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964417Shub1966416Pipes1990511Pipes19963Read2005247-537">[534]</a></sup> Ryan stated that he was "not a dictator in the sense that all his recommendations were accepted and implemented", for many of his colleagues disagreed with him on various issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan20121_538-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan20121-538">[535]</a></sup> Fischer noted that while "Lenin was a dictator, \[he was\] not the kind of dictator Stalin later became."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer1964524_539-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer1964524-539">[536]</a></sup> Volkogonov believed that whereas Lenin established a "dictatorship of the Party", it would only be under Stalin that the Soviet Union became the "dictatorship of one man."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994313_540-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994313-540">[537]</a></sup> [Moshe Lewin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moshe_Lewin "Moshe Lewin") presented a differing view and argued that "The Soviet regime underwent a long period of "Stalinism", which in its basic features was diametrically opposed to the recommendations of \[Lenin's\] testament".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewin2005136_541-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewin2005136-541">[538]</a></sup>

Conversely, various Marxist observers, including Western historians Hill and [John Rees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Rees_(activist) "John Rees (activist)"), argued against the view that Lenin's government was a dictatorship, viewing it instead as an imperfect way of preserving elements of democracy without some of the processes found in liberal democratic states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003120_542-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003120-542">[539]</a></sup> Ryan contends that the leftist historian [Paul Le Blanc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Le_Blanc_(historian) "Paul Le Blanc (historian)") "makes a quite valid point that the personal qualities that led Lenin to brutal policies were not necessarily any stronger than in some of the major Western leaders of the twentieth century."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan2012191_543-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan2012191-543">[540]</a></sup> Ryan also posits that for Lenin revolutionary violence was merely a means to an end, namely the establishment of a socialist, ultimately communist world—a world without violence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan2012184_544-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan2012184-544">[541]</a></sup> Historian [J. Arch Getty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._Arch_Getty "J. Arch Getty") remarked, "Lenin deserves a lot of credit for the notion that the meek can inherit the earth, that there can be a political movement based on social justice and equality."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Biography''_545-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Biography''-545">[542]</a></sup> Some left-wing intellectuals, among them [Slavoj Žižek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavoj_%C5%BDi%C5%BEek "Slavoj Žižek"), [Alain Badiou](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alain_Badiou "Alain Badiou"), Lars T. Lih, and [Fredric Jameson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fredric_Jameson "Fredric Jameson"), advocate reviving Lenin's uncompromising revolutionary spirit to address contemporary global problems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan20123BudgenKouvelakisŽižek20071–4_546-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan20123BudgenKouvelakis%C5%BDi%C5%BEek20071%E2%80%934-546">[543]</a></sup>

### Within the Soviet Union

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Russia-2007-Moscow-Kremlin_Senate_at_night.jpg/220px-Russia-2007-Moscow-Kremlin_Senate_at_night.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Russia-2007-Moscow-Kremlin_Senate_at_night.jpg)

Lenin's Mausoleum in front of the Kremlin, 2007

In the Soviet Union, a cult of personality devoted to Lenin began to develop during his lifetime, but was only fully established after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994327Tumarkin19972White2001185Read2005260_547-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994327Tumarkin19972White2001185Read2005260-547">[544]</a></sup> According to historian Nina Tumarkin, it represented the world's "most elaborate cult of a revolutionary leader" since that of [George Washington](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Washington "George Washington") in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETumarkin19972_548-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTETumarkin19972-548">[545]</a></sup> and has been repeatedly described as "quasi-religious" in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes1990814Service2000485White2001185Petrovsky-Shtern2010114Read2005284_549-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes1990814Service2000485White2001185Petrovsky-Shtern2010114Read2005284-549">[546]</a></sup> Busts or statues of Lenin were erected in almost every village,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994328_550-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994328-550">[547]</a></sup> and his face adorned postage stamps, crockery, posters, and the front pages of Soviet newspapers _Pravda_ and _[Izvestia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Izvestia "Izvestia")_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000486_551-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000486-551">[548]</a></sup> The places where he had lived or stayed were converted into museums devoted to him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994328_550-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994328-550">[547]</a></sup> Libraries, streets, farms, museums, towns, and whole regions were named after him,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994328_550-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994328-550">[547]</a></sup> with the city of Petrograd being renamed "Leningrad" in 1924,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994437Service2000482_552-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994437Service2000482-552">[549]</a></sup> and his birthplace of Simbirsk becoming Ulyanovsk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELih201122_553-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELih201122-553">[550]</a></sup> The [Order of Lenin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_Lenin "Order of Lenin") was established as one of the country's highest decorations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000486_551-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000486-551">[548]</a></sup> All of this was contrary to Lenin's own desires and was publicly criticised by his widow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994440_510-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994440-510">[507]</a></sup>

Various biographers have stated that Lenin's writings were treated in a manner akin to [holy scripture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_scripture "Holy scripture") within the Soviet Union,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShub1966439Pipes19961Service2000482_554-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShub1966439Pipes19961Service2000482-554">[551]</a></sup> while Pipes added that "his every opinion was cited to justify one policy or another and treated as gospel."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes19961_555-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes19961-555">[552]</a></sup> Stalin systematised Leninism through a series of lectures at the [Sverdlov University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sverdlov_University "Sverdlov University"), which were then published as _Questions of Leninism_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000484White2001185Read2005260,_284_556-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000484White2001185Read2005260,_284-556">[553]</a></sup> Stalin also had much of the deceased leader's writings collated and stored in a secret archive in the [Marx–Engels–Lenin Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%E2%80%93Engels%E2%80%93Lenin_Institute "Marx–Engels–Lenin Institute").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994274–275_557-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994274%E2%80%93275-557">[554]</a></sup> Material such as Lenin's collection of books in Kraków was also collected from abroad for storage in the institute, often at great expense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994262_558-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994262-558">[555]</a></sup> During the Soviet era, these writings were strictly controlled and very few had access.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994261_559-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994261-559">[556]</a></sup> All of Lenin's writings that proved useful to Stalin were published, but the others remained hidden,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994263_560-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994263-560">[557]</a></sup> and knowledge of both Lenin's non-Russian ancestry and his noble status was suppressed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000486_551-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000486-551">[548]</a></sup> In particular, knowledge of his Jewish ancestry was suppressed until the 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern201099Lih201120_561-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern201099Lih201120-561">[558]</a></sup> perhaps out of Soviet antisemitism,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead20056_562-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead20056-562">[559]</a></sup> and so as not to undermine Stalin's Russification efforts,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern2010108_563-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern2010108-563">[560]</a></sup> and perhaps so as not to provide fuel for anti-Soviet sentiment among international antisemites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead20056_562-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead20056-562">[559]</a></sup> After the discovery of Lenin's Jewish ancestry, this aspect was repeatedly emphasised by the Russian far-right, who claimed that his inherited Jewish genetics explained his desire to uproot traditional Russian society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern2010134,_159–161_564-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrovsky-Shtern2010134,_159%E2%80%93161-564">[561]</a></sup> Under Stalin's regime, Lenin was actively portrayed as a close friend of Stalin's who had supported Stalin's bid to be the next Soviet leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000485_565-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000485-565">[562]</a></sup> During the Soviet era, five separate editions of Lenin's published works were published in Russian, the first beginning in 1920 and the last from 1958 to 1965; the fifth edition was described as "complete", but in reality, had much omitted for political expediency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes19961–2White2001183_566-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes19961%E2%80%932White2001183-566">[563]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/URSS_1_rublo_centenario_nascita_Lenin.JPG/220px-URSS_1_rublo_centenario_nascita_Lenin.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:URSS_1_rublo_centenario_nascita_Lenin.JPG)

Commemorative [one ruble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_ruble "Soviet ruble") coin minted in 1970 in honour of the centenary of Lenin's birth

After Stalin's death, [Nikita Khrushchev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev "Nikita Khrushchev") became leader of the Soviet Union and began a process of [de-Stalinisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De-Stalinisation "De-Stalinisation"), citing Lenin's writings, including those on Stalin, to legitimise this process.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994452–453Service2000491–492Lee2003131_567-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVolkogonov1994452%E2%80%93453Service2000491%E2%80%93492Lee2003131-567">[564]</a></sup> When [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev") took power in 1985 and introduced the policies of _[glasnost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasnost "Glasnost")_ and _[perestroika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perestroika "Perestroika")_, he too cited these actions as a return to Lenin's principles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEService2000491–492_568-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEService2000491%E2%80%93492-568">[565]</a></sup> In late 1991, amid the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union"), Russian President [Boris Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin "Boris Yeltsin") ordered the Lenin archive be removed from Communist Party control and placed under the control of a state organ, the [Russian Centre for the Preservation and Study of Documents of Recent History](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_State_Archive_of_Contemporary_History "Russian State Archive of Contemporary History"), at which it was revealed that over 6,000 of Lenin's writings had gone unpublished. These were declassified and made available for scholarly study.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPipes19962–3_569-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPipes19962%E2%80%933-569">[566]</a></sup> Since 1991, there has been some discussion about moving Lenin's body from the [mausoleum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin_mausoleum "Lenin mausoleum") to the [Kremlin Wall Necropolis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kremlin_Wall_Necropolis "Kremlin Wall Necropolis") and burying it there. President Boris Yeltsin, with the support of the [Russian Orthodox Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church "Russian Orthodox Church"), intended to close the mausoleum and bury Lenin next to his mother, [Maria Alexandrovna Ulyanova](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maria_Alexandrovna_Ulyanova "Maria Alexandrovna Ulyanova"), at the [Volkov Cemetery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volkov_Cemetery "Volkov Cemetery") in [St. Petersburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Petersburg "St. Petersburg"). His successor, [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin"), opposed this, stating that a reburial of Lenin would imply that generations of citizens had observed false values during seventy years of Soviet rule.<sup id="cite_ref-570"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-570">[567]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-571"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-571">[568]</a></sup>

In Russia in 2012, a proposal from a deputy belonging to the [Liberal Democratic Party of Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberal_Democratic_Party_of_Russia "Liberal Democratic Party of Russia"), with the support of some members of the governing [United Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Russia "United Russia") party, proposed the removal of Lenin monuments in Russia. The proposal was strongly opposed by the [Communist Party of the Russian Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Russian_Federation "Communist Party of the Russian Federation") and was never considered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''The_Moscow_Times'',_24_October_2013_572-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''The_Moscow_Times'',_24_October_2013-572">[569]</a></sup> Russia retained the vast majority of the 7,000 Lenin statues extant in 1991; as of 2022, there were approximately 6,000 monuments to Lenin in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-573"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-573">[570]</a></sup>

In Ukraine, during the 2013–14 [Euromaidan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euromaidan "Euromaidan") protests, Lenin statues were [damaged or destroyed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demolition_of_monuments_to_Vladimir_Lenin_in_Ukraine "Demolition of monuments to Vladimir Lenin in Ukraine") by protesters in various cities across the country,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC,_22_February_2014_574-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC,_22_February_2014-574">[571]</a></sup> and in April 2015 the Ukrainian government ordered that all others be dismantled to comply with [decommunisation laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decommunization_in_Ukraine "Decommunization in Ukraine").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC,_14_April_2015_575-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC,_14_April_2015-575">[572]</a></sup> During the [Russia's full-scale invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine") in 2022, many Lenin statues which had been taken down by Ukrainian activists in the preceding years, were [re-erected](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_imagery_during_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "Soviet imagery during the Russo-Ukrainian War") by Russian occupiers in [Russian-controlled areas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian-occupied_territories_of_Ukraine "Russian-occupied territories of Ukraine"). These actions have less to do with communist propaganda and more with Lenin symbolizing Russia's domination over Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-576"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-576">[573]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-577"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-577">[574]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-578"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-578">[575]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-579"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-579">[576]</a></sup>

### In the international communist movement

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Detalle_de_Lenin.jpg/220px-Detalle_de_Lenin.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Detalle_de_Lenin.jpg)

Detail of _[Man, Controller of the Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Man,_Controller_of_the_Universe "Man, Controller of the Universe")_, a fresco by [Diego Rivera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diego_Rivera "Diego Rivera") in the [Palacio de Bellas Artes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palacio_de_Bellas_Artes "Palacio de Bellas Artes") in [Mexico City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico_City "Mexico City"), depicting Lenin

According to Lenin biographer [David Shub](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Shub "David Shub"), writing in 1965, it was Lenin's ideas and example that "constitutes the basis of the Communist movement today."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShub196610_580-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShub196610-580">[577]</a></sup> Socialist states following Lenin's ideas appeared in various parts of the world during the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan20125_527-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan20125-527">[524]</a></sup> Writing in 1972, the historian [Marcel Liebman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcel_Liebman "Marcel Liebman") stated that "there is hardly any insurrectionary movement today, from Latin America to Angola, that does not lay claim to the heritage of Leninism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiebman197522_581-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiebman197522-581">[578]</a></sup>

After Lenin's death, Stalin's administration established an ideology known as [Marxism–Leninism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism "Marxism–Leninism"), a movement that came to be interpreted differently by various contending factions in the communist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShub19669Service2000482_582-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShub19669Service2000482-582">[579]</a></sup> After being forced into exile by Stalin's administration, Trotsky argued that Stalinism was a debasement of Leninism, which was dominated by bureaucratism and Stalin's own personal dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003132_583-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003132-583">[580]</a></sup>

Marxism–Leninism was adapted to many of the 20th century's most prominent revolutionary movements, forming into variants such as [Stalinism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stalinism "Stalinism"), [Maoism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maoism "Maoism"), [Juche](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juche "Juche"), [Ho Chi Minh Thought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_Thought "Ho Chi Minh Thought"), and [Castroism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castroism "Castroism").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead2005283_526-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead2005283-526">[523]</a></sup> Conversely, many later Western communists, such as [Manuel Azcárate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manuel_Azc%C3%A1rate "Manuel Azcárate") and Jean Ellenstein, who were involved in the [Eurocommunist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurocommunist "Eurocommunist") movement, expressed the view that Lenin and his ideas were irrelevant to their own objectives, thereby embracing a Marxist but not Marxist–Leninist perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003132–133_584-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003132%E2%80%93133-584">[581]</a></sup>
